class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">168</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">We report the fabrication of 3D flower-like MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets arrays on carbon cloth as a binder-free anode for sodium ion battery. Ultrathin and conformal TiO<sub>2</sub> layers are used to modify the surface of MoS<sub>2</sub> by atomic layer deposition. The electrochemical performance measurements demonstrate that the ALD TiO<sub>2</sub> layer can improve the cycling stability and rate capability of MoS<sub>2</sub>. The MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with 0.5-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> coating electrode show the highest initial discharge capacity of 1392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which is increased by 53% compared with that of bare MoS<sub>2</sub>. After 150 cycles, the capacity retention rate of the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> achieves 75.8% of its second cycle’s capacity at 200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in contrast to that of 59% of pure MoS<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the mechanism behind the experimental results is revealed by ex situ scanning electron microscope (SEM), X-ray powder diffraction (XRD), and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) characterizations, which confirms that the ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub> modifications can prevent the structural degradation and the formation of SEI film of MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D487%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWeina%2BRen%252C%2BWeiwei%2BZhou%252C%2BHaifeng%2BZhang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b13179%26title%3DALD%2BTiO2-Coated%2BFlower-like%2BMoS2%2BNanosheets%2Bon%2BCarbon%2BCloth%2Bas%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBattery%2BAnode%2Bwith%2BEnhanced%2BCycling%2BStability%2Band%2BRate%2BCapability%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D495%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers"> Layers </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides"> Oxides </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes"> Physical and chemical processes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20battery">sodium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Atomic+Layer+Deposition&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_atomic%20layer%20deposition">atomic layer deposition</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Flower-like+Mos2+Nanosheets&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_flower-like%20MoS2%20nanosheets">flower-like MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Tio2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_TiO2">TiO<sub>2</sub></a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have received increasing attention as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries (LIB) due to the low cost, natural abundance, and similar redox potential to lithium of sodium element. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> However, the selection and exploration of suitable electrode materials for Na-ion batteries is still a great challenge due to the fact that Na<sup>+</sup> has a larger radius (1.06 Å) than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), leading to a higher volume expansion and lower energy densities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Currently, the investigated anode materials for sodium ion batteries include carbon-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5-7)</a> alloy-based materials, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8-10)</a> metal oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11-13)</a> and transition metal sulfides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14-16)</a> Among them, MoS<sub>2</sub>, as a member of layer transition metal dichalcogenides, is a promising candidate anode because of its graphite-like structure with large layer spacing of 0.615 nm and high theoretical capacities (670 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> However, the poor cycling stability and rates capability arising from the low electronic transmission rate and aggregation tendency plus the dissolution of reaction intermediates (Na<sub>2</sub>S) during charging/discharging remain stumbling blocks on the road to development of MoS<sub>2</sub> anode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18-20)</a> To address these problems, one effective approach is preparation of ultrathin and expanded spacing MoS<sub>2</sub> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21, 22)</a> and combination with high conductivity materials such as carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 14 and 23 to 26">(14, 23-26)</a> In addition, deposition of a passivation layer at the electrode/electrolyte interface is another effective strategy to prevent the structure degradation and repeated formation of SEI films, which have been widely adopted in LIBs research <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27, 28)</a> and also NIBs anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29, 30)</a> To this end, atomic layer deposition shows great potential for thin film deposition and electrode surface modification due to its advantages in excellent shape retention and thickness controllability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 31 to 36">(31-36)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, we report for the first time the preparation of ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub>-modified 3D flower-like MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets arrays on flexible carbon cloth by a hydrothermal method and a subsequent ALD process. As a binder-free anode for sodium ion battery, such an electrode design is favorable to improve the specific capacity, cycling stability, and rates performance. First, the 3D flower-like nanosheets arrays directly supported on a current collector with large surface area facilitate the electron and ion diffusion and transportation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>). Second, the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer is stable enough during sodiation/desodiation cycles, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> which can act as a satisfactory passivation and buffer layer of MoS<sub>2</sub> to prevent the electrode corrosion and collapse. Moreover, the synergistic effect between the MoS<sub>2</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub> is conducive to improving the electrode capacity and cycling stability. By testing them as NIBs anodes, enhanced capacity, cycling stability, and rates capability are demonstrated by ALD TiO<sub>2</sub> modification on MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets surface.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0009.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Diagram for the Description of Na<sup>+</sup> Insertion and e<sup>–</sup> Diffusion Processes</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1 Sample Preparation</h3><div id="sec2_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Synthesis of 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> Nanosheets on Carbon Cloth</h4><div class="NLM_p last">Carbon cloth was cleaned in acetone, ethanol, and deionized water by ultrasonication for 20 min each, respectively, and then soaked in nitric acid with a concentration of 65% for 24 h to improve its surface hydrophilicity. The growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on carbon cloth was then accomplished by a hydrothermal method. In a typical process 120 mg of sodium molybdate (NaMoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O) and 190 mg of thiourea (CH<sub>4</sub>N<sub>2</sub>S) were dissolved in 30 mL of deionized water and stirred for 30 min until clear, and then the above solution was transferred to a 50-mL sealed Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave with one piece of the treated carbon cloth placed inside. The temperature was kept at 200 °C by an electric oven and maintained for 20 h. After that, the sample was taken out and washed with deionized water repeatedly, then dried in an oven at 80 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> ALD of Ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub> Layer on MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets</h4><div class="NLM_p last">The ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub> layer coating on the MoS<sub>2</sub> surface with different thickness was conducted by a Picosun R-200 ALD system. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> During the deposition process, TiCl<sub>4</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O were used as the precursors of Ti and O, respectively. The TiO<sub>2</sub> was deposited at 75 °C with the speed of ∼0.8 Å/cycle and the thickness of the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer was effectively controlled through different cycles’ settings: 7 cycles, 13 cycles, and 25 cycles were used to deposit about 0.5, 1, and 2 nm, respectively.</div></div></div><div id="sec2_1_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.2 Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The microscopic structure and morphology of the as-synthesized MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were recorded by field-emission scanning electron microscope (FE-SEM, FEI Sirion200), high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HR-TEM, JEM-2010F), and selected area diffraction patterns. The crystal structure, composition, and valence state of the samples were characterized by X-ray powder diffraction (XRD, Bruker D8-Advance) and X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS, Kratos Axis Ultra DLD).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical measurements of all the active materials were carried out by using CR2032 coin-type cells. MoS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on carbon cloth were directly used as binder-free anodes, and sodium foil (J&amp;K Scientific) was used as the cathode for sodium ion battery. A microporous glass fiber membrane (Whatman) and 1 M solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1 by weight) with 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC)were employed as the separator and electrolyte for the cells, respectively. The carbon’s density was 13.4 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> and the average loading density of these active materials was about 3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The galvanostatic charge–discharge cycles at different current densities and the rates performance were tested in a multichannel battery tester (Neware Co., China) with a voltage of 0.01–2.5 V at room temperature. The cyclic voltammetry measurements were performed on a CHI760D electrochemical workstation.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3 Results and Discussions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.1 Morphology and Structure Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and structure of the as-obtained MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated ones were characterized by SEM and XRD. The low-magnification SEM image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows that spherical MoS<sub>2</sub> structures with textured surfaces are almost uniformly wrapped around each carbon fiber. From the closer view in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b, it can be seen that the flower-like MoS<sub>2</sub> structures are assembled of several thin nanosheets. The diameter of each flower is about 1 μm. After the ALD of 2-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> coating, the morphology does not change much, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, which is attributed to the characteristics of ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub> layer and ultrauniform deposition characteristics of ALD. The SEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets coated with 0.5 and 1 nm of TiO<sub>2</sub> are also provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information Figure S1</a>. The EDX elemental mapping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b–e</a>) images obtained from the MoS<sub>2</sub>-2 nm TiO<sub>2</sub> sample indicate the good distribution of the Mo, S, Ti, and O elements. The phase purity and crystallinity of the as-prepared samples were further demonstrated by XRD. As displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, all the diffraction peaks are matched with the hexagonal phase of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS 37-1492). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39, 40)</a> The peaks at 14.2°, 33°, 40°, 44°, and 58° are respectively indexed to (002), (100), (103), (006), and (110) planes of the single-phase MoS<sub>2</sub>, and the peak at 26° comes from the carbon cloth substrate. Especially, the (002) peak at 14.2° indicates the stacked layered structure of the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Noting that we have not observed the characteristic peaks of TiO<sub>2</sub> in the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated sample, which is due to the amorphous structure of TiO<sub>2</sub> deposited at 75 °C.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SEM images of (a, b) 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, (c) MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets coated with 2 nm of TiO<sub>2</sub>, and (d) XRD patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To explore more detailed structural information on the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>–TiO<sub>2</sub> composites, TEM and HR-TEM observations were performed. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, the ultrathin MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are interlaced with each other to form the flowers, which is consistent with the SEM observations. From the HR-TEM image of the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, we can easily observe the lamellar structure on the edge of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet with a thickness of about 9 nm, which is corresponding to 13–15 S–Mo–S layers with an interlayer spacing of about 0.635 nm. The thicknesses of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are between 5 and 10 nm, which can be clearly observed from the TEM image in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The corresponding SAED patterns in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c demonstrate that the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are polycrystalline. The diffraction rings are accurately indexed to (110), (103), (100), and (002) planes of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>. The HR-TEM picture of the MoS<sub>2</sub> with 2-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> coating is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, which confirms that the amorphous TiO<sub>2</sub> layer is uniformly coated the outside of the multilayer structure, and the thickness is precisely controlled at about 2 nm by ALD.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) TEM image, (b) HRTEM image, and (c) SAED patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets; (d) HRTEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The composition and chemical state of the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> sample was further investigated by XPS. The high-resolution XPS spectra in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> show narrow range scans for the peaks of the four elements. The high-resolution Mo 3d XPS spectrum in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows two peaks with binding energies of 232.8 and 229.6 eV, which correspond to Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively. The peak at 236 eV is attributed to the Mo<sup>6+</sup> 3d, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> which might be due to the oxidation of Mo<sup>4+</sup> in the atmosphere. The S 2s peak is detected at 226.8 eV. The S<sup>2–</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S<sup>2–</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b are centered at 162.3 and 163.5 eV, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41, 42)</a> As show in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c, there are two peaks at 464.7 and 458.9, which can be attributed to Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The O 1s XPS spectrum is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d; besides the O 1s peaks at 530 eV attributed to the Ti–O–Ti bond, the peak at 532.0 eV could be assigned to the adsorbed water. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44, 45)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. High-resolution XPS spectra of the (a) Mo 3d peak, (b) S 2p peak, (c) Ti 2p peak, and (d) O 1s peak.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of as-synthesized MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated ones with different thickness were evaluated by tested them as sodium ion battery anodes. First, we carried out the cyclic voltammetry measurements for each electrode at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.001 and 2.5 V. In the first cycle, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, the curves of all the electrodes show typical peaks of MoS<sub>2</sub> in the reduction and oxidation reactions. The first reduction peak at ∼1.9 V attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into MoS<sub>2</sub>, forming Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> are observed in pure MoS<sub>2</sub>, and 0.5- and 1-nm TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Noting that this peak intensity decreases with the increase of TiO<sub>2</sub> layer thickness until disappearing in the 2-nm TiO<sub>2</sub>-deposited MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, which might be due to that the thick TiO<sub>2</sub> layer would prevent the sodium ion to diffuse and react with the MoS<sub>2</sub> in a timely manner. The following reduction peaks of all the electrodes at ∼0.8 V and ∼0.6 V are relevant to the further insertion of Na ions in MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref46" aria-label="reference 25 and 46">(25, 46)</a> The peak under 0.3 V in the deep cathodic process corresponds to the subsequent conversion reaction from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Mo. The corresponding oxidation peaks at about 0.4, 1.8, and 2.3 V are also observed. In the second cycle in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b of CV curves, the reduction peaks at ∼0.8 V and ∼0.6 V are reduced and change to higher potential of 0.7 and 1.4 V, respectively. One peak at 1.4 V is attributed to the intercalation reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and the other one at 0.7 V is assigned to the conversion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The redox peaks tend to keep stable in the following four cycles as displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, which indicates the stable and reversible process of sodiation/desodiation reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46-48)</a> During the oxidation/reduction process, the typical peaks of TiO<sub>2</sub> are not observed, which may be due to the ultrathin thickness of TiO<sub>2</sub> layer.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) First cycle and (b) second cycle of CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes measured at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the charge–discharge voltage-capacity curves of all the samples for the first cycle at the current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and a potential window of 0.01–2.5 V. The three voltage plateaus located at 0.8, 0.6, and 0.2–0.01 V are observed in the four electrodes, which are consistent with the CV analysis in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. To prove the stable cycling performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub> modified electrode, the galvanostatic charge–discharge test was continued until 150 cycles under the same condition, which is demonstrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. According to the order of TiO<sub>2</sub> thickness from thin to thick, the initial discharge and charge capacities of each electrode are 1392.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 957.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (0.5 nm), 1101 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 751.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (1 nm), and 1147.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 745.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (2 nm). Compared with that of the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> (910 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and 596.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), the initial discharge capacity of the electrodes modified by TiO<sub>2</sub> was significantly improved, and the initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) also increased from 64% to 69%. After 150 cycles, the discharge specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub> is 317.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is only 59% of its second cycle’s capacity. Meanwhile, the capacity retention rates of the composite electrodes with 0.5-, 1-, and 2-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> coating are 75.8%, 74.1%, and 64%, respectively. This performance improvement in TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> might be attributed to the synergistic effect of the composite materials and the passivation nature of TiO<sub>2</sub> layer with functions of preventing pulverization of MoS<sub>2</sub> caused by the volume change during charging/discharging process, as well as protecting the MoS<sub>2</sub> from corrosion and preventing the formation of SEI film.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Charge–discharge profiles of initial cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (b) cycling performances at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate of electrode at various current densities of 50, 100, 200, 400, and 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively; (d) cycling performances at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for the electrodes of MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c exhibits the rates performance of the samples: the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets electrodes with ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub> modification show outstanding capacity retention at various current densities from 50 to 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at a high rate of 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, their capacities retention could be still preserved at more than 42%, which corresponds to the capacities of 392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, 369 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and 329 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. When the current returns to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes showed the specific capacity of 801 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 754 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and 775 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Their capacity recovery rates are as high as 92% or more, while that of the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> is only 71%. The capacity and cycling performance of the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes in our case are superior to most of the previously reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composite electrodes, as summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">To verify the superiority of TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the long-term stability of the samples was further evaluated at a high current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. After 200 cycles, the reversible capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes could be still maintained at 182 and 157 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and the corresponding capacity retentions were 60% and 69%. These capacities retention are far higher than that of bare and 0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, which are only 40% and 33%. From the view of this, the cycle stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode at high rate is not ideal. Such a similar poor cyclic performance with MoS<sub>2</sub> might be due to the fact that the 0.5-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> layer is too thin to adapt to the rapid volume change during sodium ion intercalation/deintercalation, leading to severe structural damage and sulfur dissolution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Taking into account the capacity performance and the cycle stability, in can be concluded that the comprehensive performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode is the best among the three electrodes with different thicknesses of TiO<sub>2</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.3 Mechanism Investigation</h3><div class="NLM_p">The mechanism behind the high rate performance and exceptional cycling stability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/TiO<sub>2</sub> anode was also investigated. The morphology structure of electrode material after cycling can directly reflect its structural stability. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a–d present the SEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> coated with 0.5-, 1-, and 2-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes after 150 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. For the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> sample, the flower-like structure completely disappears after several cycles due to serious collapse and agglomeration problems. Whereas for the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated ones, the flower-like structures are kept well, demonstrating the protective effect of the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer. The TEM images of bare MoS<sub>2</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> samples after 5 charging–discharging cycles are also shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. No SEI films were observed in the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated electrode in contrast to that of bare MoS<sub>2</sub> one, indicating that the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer modification can effectively prevent the formation of SEI films. On the asis of the above discussions, the high capacity retention characteristic of the ALD TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes might be attributed to the function of ultrathin TiO<sub>2</sub> layer that can buffer the volume change of MoS<sub>2</sub> during charging/discharging process and act as a protective barrier layer between the anode/electrolyte interface to prevent the formation of SEI film, as a result of keeping the structural integrity.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. SEM images of  3D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets after 150 cycles at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>: (a) uncoated; (b) coated with 0.5-nm TiO<sub>2</sub>; (c) coated with 1-nm TiO<sub>2</sub>; (d) coated with 2-nm TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the ex situ XRD patterns of all the electrodes after 100 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> were measured to study the structure and phase change after sodium ion intercalation/deintercalation process. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>, from the bottom to up, the diffraction patterns correspond to the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> (I), MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> (II), MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> (III), and MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> (IV), respectively. After several cycles, two new additional peaks at 9.46° and 19.2° are clearly observed in the TiO<sub>2</sub>-coated samples, which correspond to the estimated lattice spacings of 9.33 and 4.61 Å, respectively. The dual relationship between these two lattice spacings demonstrates that a new lamellar structure with an enlarged interlayer spacing was formed during the process of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The diffraction peaks of composite material at 32.3°, 43.4°, and 56.8° indexed to the (100), (006), and (110) planes of MoS<sub>2</sub> were also observed. These results suggest that the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer can protect the crystal structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> very well. However, the peak of corresponding (002) plane does not appear in the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, which indicates that the layered structure was severely damaged during the cycles without the protection of TiO<sub>2</sub>. The XRD results are consistent with the analyses of SEM in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. XRD patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets (I) and MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets coated with 0.5-nm (II), 1-nm (III) and 2-nm (IV) TiO<sub>2</sub> after 150 cycles at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To understand why the MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode and MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibit different long cycling stability at varied current density, impedance measurements of the two samples were carried out after 50 cycles at a constant current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and after 100 cycles at a constant current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a and b, all the Nyquist plots were consisted of two semicircles at high and medium frequencies and straight sloping lines at low frequencies. It is well-known that the intercept of the Z′ axis in the high frequency corresponds to the bulk resistance of electrolyte, separator, and electrodes, the high frequency semicircle corresponds to the resistance of SEI film, the medium frequency semicircle corresponds to the charge-transfer resistance, and the low frequency sloping line is associated with the diffusion of sodium ions in the active material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 49 to 52">(49-52)</a> The corresponding Nyquist plots was fitted by an equivalent circuit model as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a, it can be seen that the semicircles at high and medium frequencies of MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode are smaller than that of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, which reveals that the MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode possesses the lower interfacial resistance after 50 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Obviously, when the current density changes to 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b, the interface resistance of MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode is much larger than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, which is arising from the collapse of structures and the absence of electrical contact. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c and d show the Z′−ω<sup>–1/2</sup> (ω = 2πf) curves in the low frequency region, and the low slope indicates good sodium ion kinetics in the electrode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 14, 50 and 51">(14, 50, 51)</a> They clearly show that the sodium ion kinetics of MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> was better than that of MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> after cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and worse than it after cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. This result is consistent with the phenomenon of the above long-term galvanostatic charge–discharge test, and the lower interface resistance and better sodium ion kinetics are the main factors leading to the excellent cycling stability.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13179h_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0008.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Nyquist plots of MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes: (a) after 50 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and (b) after 100 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Linear fits in low frequency region of the Nyquist plots (c) after 50 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) after 100 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/asset/images/large/am-2016-13179h_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13179&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, 3D flower-like MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets arrays on carbon cloth have been fabricated by a facile and scalable hydrothermal route as binder-free anode for sodium ion batteries. The cycling stability and rate capability of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets electrodes were improved greatly by ALD deposited conformal TiO<sub>2</sub> layer. After 150 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, a remarkable capacity retention characteristic (75.8% of second capacity) was demonstrated for the TiO<sub>2</sub> coated electrode. Such an excellent electrochemical performance is ascribed to the functional TiO<sub>2</sub> layer that can keep the structural integrity of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and prevent SEI layer formation. The ex-situ SEM and XRD results revealed that the TiO<sub>2</sub> layer is able to improve the crystallinity and 3D structure retention of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets during cycling process, and the EIS tests showed the relatively thick TiO<sub>2</sub> layer can reduce the resistance of SEI film and the electron-transfer, and promote the diffusion efficiency of the sodium ion at a higher current density. Our study provides a valuable reference for the electrode design and surface engineering toward high-performance sodium ion batteries with long cycling life and high rate capability.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b13179" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b13179</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets coated with 0.5- and 1-nm TiO<sub>2</sub> layer; EDX mapping of MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>; TEM images of 3D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets; CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>–0.5-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, MoS<sub>2</sub>–1-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the first 5 cycles; TEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and MoS<sub>2</sub>–2-nm-TiO<sub>2</sub> after 5 cycles; equivalent circuit diagram of the Nyquist plots; table of comparison of electrochemical performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13179/suppl_file/am6b13179_si_001.pdf">am6b13179_si_001.pdf (257.1 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b13179" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201206854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cheng, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Goodenough, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium manganese hexacyanoferrate cathode materials with two structures can be synthesized at room temp.  The sodium-ion concn. in the aq. soln. during pptn. has a strong influence on the compn. and structure of the final product, resulting in different reversible capacity and cycling performance.  With higher Na+ concn., a cooperative Na+ displacement along a single [111] direction induces a transition from cubic to rhombohedral symmetry - this phase transition is reversible on cycling the Na+ concn.  Sodium manganese hexacyanoferrate with an initial rhombohedral structure exhibits a reversible capacity of 134 mA-h/g and retains 120 mA-h/g after 30 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Research interest in Na-ion batteries has increased rapidly because of the environmental friendliness of sodium compared to lithium.  A review of recent scientific advances in the field of neg. electrode materials used for Na-ion batteries.  This paper sheds light on neg. electrode materials for Na-ion batteries: carbonaceous materials, oxides/phosphates (as sodium insertion materials), sodium alloy/compds. and so on.  These electrode materials have different reaction mechanisms for electrochem. sodiation/desodiation processes.  Moreover, not only sodiation-active materials but also binders, current collectors, electrolytes and electrode/electrolyte interphase and its stabilization are essential for long cycle life Na-ion batteries.  This paper also addresses the prospect of Na-ion batteries as low-cost and long-life batteries with relatively high-energy d. as their potential competitive edge over the commercialized Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5033"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wen, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Matsuda, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ishii, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cumings, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Expanded Graphite as Superior Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4033</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1038/nnano.2015.194"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, H.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pasta, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zheng, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Phosphorene-Graphene Hybrid Material as a High-Capacity Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nat.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA04370D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kong, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cheng, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Seed-assisted growth of α-Fe2O3 nanorod arrays on reduced graphene oxide: a superior anode for high-performance Li-ion and Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kong, Dezhi; Cheng, Chuanwei; Wang, Ye; Liu, Bo; Huang, Zhixiang; Yang, Hui Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">α-Fe2O3 nanorod/reduced graphene oxide nanosheet composites (denoted as α-Fe2O3@r-GO NRAs) are fabricated by using a facile and scalable seed-assisted hydrothermal growth route, in which the α-Fe2O3 nanorods are assembled onto the side surfaces of r-GO nanosheets.  Such α-Fe2O3@r-GO hybrid nanostructures are tested as anodes for both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries (LIBs and SIBs), which exhibit excellent performance with high capacity and long-cycling stability.  When used for LIBs, the hybrid α-Fe2O3@r-GO NRAs electrode exhibits a highly stable Li+ storage capacity of 1200 mA h g-1 after 500 cycles at 0.2C and excellent rate capability.  Moreover, the hybrid α-Fe2O3@r-GO NRAs also display their versatility as an anode for SIBs, which delivers high reversible Na+ storage capacity of 332 mA h g-1 at 0.2C over 300 cycles with long-term cycling stability.  The excellent electrochem. performance of the hybrid α-Fe2O3@r-GO NRAs anodes could be ascribed to the synergistic effect between the α-Fe2O3 nanorod arrays and reduced graphene oxide nanosheets, which could availably promote the charge transport and accommodate the vol. change upon the long-term charge-discharge process for reversible Li+ or Na+ storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C4NR05604C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Walter, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kravchyk, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report a facile chem. synthesis of monodisperse colloidal SnSb nanocrystals (NCs) via reaction between Sn NCs and SbCl3 in oleylamine under reducing conditions.  In comparison with individual Sn and Sb NCs and their mixts., we show that through the creation of SnSb alloyed NCs the Li-ion storage properties are enhanced due to combination of high cycling stability of Sb with higher specific Li-ion storage capacity of Sn.         </div><div class="casAbstract">For the first time, cycling capability and life anal. of the FeSb2/Na battery are tested.  Thanks to an appropriate CM-cellulose/carbon formulation, this electrode exhibits excellent electrochem. performances as neg. electrode material for Sodium-Ion Batteries (SIB), sustaining a reversible capacity exceeding 540 and 440 mAh/g over more than 130 cycles at a current of 36 and 300 mA/g, resp. Rational design and controllable synthesis of nano-heterostructure anode materials with high-rate performance, however, still remains a great challenge.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Mixed metal sulfide composite microspheres with a yolk-shell structure for Na-ion batteries were studied.  Sn-Mo oxide yolk-shell microspheres prepd. by a 1-pot spray pyrolysis process transform into yolk-shell SnS-MoS2 composite microspheres.  The discharge capacities of the yolk-shell and dense-structured SnS-MoS2 composite microspheres for the 100th cycle are 396 and 207 mA h g-1, and their capacity retentions measured from the 2nd cycle are 89 and 47%, resp.  The yolk-shell SnS-MoS2 composite microspheres with high structural stability during repeated Na insertion and desertion processes have low charge-transfer resistance even after long-term cycling.  The synergetic effect of the yolk-shell structure and uniform mixing of the SnS and MoS2 nanocrystals result in the excellent Na-ion storage properties of the yolk-shell SnS-MoS2 composite microspheres by improving their structural stability during cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201402563"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion energy storage, including sodium-ion batteries and electrochem. capacitive storage, is considered as a promising alternative to lithium-ion energy storage.  It is an intriguing prospect, esp. for large-scale applications, owing to its low cost and abundance.  MoS2 sodiation/desodiation with Na ions is based on the conversion reaction, which is not only able to deliver higher capacity than the intercalation reaction, but can also be applied in capacitive storage owing to its typically sloping charge/discharge curves.  Here, sodium-ion batteries and sodium-ion pseudocapacitors based on a graphene composite (MoS2/graphene) were constructed.  The enlarged d-spacing, a contribution of the graphene matrix, and the unique properties of the MoS2/graphene substantially optimize Na storage behavior, by accommodating large vol. changes and facilitating fast ion diffusion.  MoS2/graphene exhibits a stable capacity of ∼350 mA-h/g over 200 cycles at 0.25C in half cells, and delivers a capacitance of 50 F/g over 2000 cycles at 1.5C in pseudocapacitors with a wide voltage window of 0.1-2.5 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201407898"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cheng, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tao, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">MoS<sub>2</sub> Nanoflowers with Expanded Interlayers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Three dimensional (3D) MoS2 nanoflowers are successfully synthesized by hydrothermal method.  Further, a composite of as prepd. MoS2 nanoflowers and rGO is constructed by simple ultrasonic exfoliation technique.  The crystallog. and morphol. studies have been carried out by XRD, FE-SEM, TEM, HR-TEM and EDS etc.  Here, XRD study revealed, a composite of exfoliated MoS2 with expanded spacing of (002) crystal plane and rGO can be prepd. by simple 40 min of ultrasonic treatment.  While, FE-SEM and TEM studies depict, individual MoS2 nanoflowers with an av. diam. of 200 nm are uniformly distributed throughout the rGO surface.  When tested as sodium-ion batteries anode material by applying two different potential windows, the composite demonstrates a high reversible specific capacity of 575 mAhg-1 at 100 mAg-1 in between 0.01 V-2.6 V and 218 mAhg-1 at 50 mAg-1 when discharged in a potential range of 0.4 V-2.6 V.  As per our concern, the results are one of the best obtained as compared to the earlier published one on MoS2 based SIB anode material and more importantly this material shows such an excellent reversible Na-storage capacity and good cycling stability without addn. of any expensive additive stabilizer, like fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC), in comparison to those in current literature.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201401521"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Luo, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gao, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Langrock, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mignerey, A.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An Advanced MoS2/Carbon Anode for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jingjing; Luo, Chao; Gao, Tao; Langrock, Alex; Mignerey, Alice C.; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">473-481</span>CODEN:
